Countries form HIVDR working groups generally based at the Ministry of Health. There are five surveillance elements: 1. HIVDR Early Warning Indicators (EWI) -- all ART sites, or representative ART sites, are surveyed annually for factors associated with HIVDR emergence/prevention: A. ART prescribing practices; B. Percentage of patients lost to follow-up after one year following ART start C. Retention on first-line ART at one year; D. Percentage of ART patients picking up their drugs on-time; E. Percentage of ART patients keeping their appointments on time F. Percentage of months in which there are no ART drug stock-outs. Sample size is calculated based on numbers of ART patients at the site. Targets are recommended; countries may select more stringent targets. 2. Monitoring of HIVDR prevention and associated factors at sentinel ART sites: HIVDR working groups select three - 15 sentinel sites annually and do baseline genotypes and endpoint genotypes on patients starting ART, and abstract other information from medical records associated with HIVDR prevention. 3. HIVDR transmission surveys: These surveys are each focused in one geographic area of the country and use a binominal sequential sampling technique which classifies prevalence of transmitted drug resistance for each of the three ART drug classes used in resource-limited countries as above or below two thresholds: 5% and 15%. 4. Cross-sectional surveys: A representative cross-section of ART patients on first-line ART are surveyed for viral load, and if the viral load is detectable (=ART failure), the specimen is genotyped to evaluate the resistance pattern. 5. Pediatric surveillance of initial HIVDR in children < =18 months of age: Remnant dried blood spots used for pediatric HIV diagnosis are genotyped for children who are HIV-positive, prevention-of-mother to child transmission (PMTCT) information is collected (which regimens received by child and mother) to evaluate resistance patterns associated with PMTCT and also for those children whose PMTCT status is recorded as \"unknown\". Information is used for public health action to change ART program practices at site and national level. The program provides services to more than 88 domestic newborn screening laboratories, 29 manufacturers of diagnostic products, and laboratories in more than 67 countries. NSQAP has been the only comprehensive source of essential quality assurance services for dried-blood-spot testing for more than 34 years. The VSD project was established in 1990 to monitor immunization safety and address the gaps in scientific knowledge about rare and serious adverse events following immunization. The VSD project includes a large linked database that uses administrative data sources at each MCO. Each participating site gathers data on vaccination (vaccine type, date of vaccination, concurrent vaccinations), medical outcomes (outpatient visits, inpatient visits, urgent care visits), birth data, and census data. The VSD project allows for planned immunization safety studies as well as timely investigations of hypotheses that arise from review of medical literature, reports to the Vaccine Adverse Event Reporting System (VAERS), changes in immunization schedules, or the introduction of new vaccines. The objectives of VSD are to conduct population based research on immunization safety questions; to evaluate immunization safety hypotheses that arise from medical literature, passive surveillance systems, adjustments to immunization schedules, and introduction of new vaccines; to guide national immunization policy decisions; and to partner with healthcare providers, public health officials, and others to ensure the public has the best available information regarding the risks and benefits of immunizations.", `reportFrequency`:"", `shortName`:"VSD", `specialPopulations`:"", `startDate`:"", `startDateGeneration`:"1991", `state`:"", `surveillanceDomain`:"", `surveillanceType`:"", `webResource`:"http://www.cdc.gov/vaccinesafety/Activities/VSD.html"})

CRA is a flexible all-hazards system that reduces the need for development of new applications each time there is a new event. This consolidated data forms a complete inventory supply picture which informs situational awareness during an event. These components are intended to cooperate and interoperate to improve communications and event response efficiency. Collectively, these components facilitate collection of i) aggregate and individual-level patient information, ii) available quantities of countermeasure inventory, and manufacturing capacity of the commercial drug sector during an event. When consolidated, the data from these systems can show population coverage, numbers of untreated, drug and equipment shortages, need for resupply, and more. Unlike most birth defects, where the causes are unknown, the etiology of FAS (prenatal exposure to alcohol) is known. FAS is 100% preventable if a woman does not drink alcohol during pregnancy. Despite increasing efforts to prevent alcohol exposed pregnancies, children continue to be born with disabilities resulting from prenatal alcohol exposure. Studies by the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) indicate FAS rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.5 per 1,000 live births in different areas of the United States. Other studies have estimated the rate of FAS at 0.5 to 2.0 cases per 1,000 live births. The variation in rates of FAS is likely a reflection of variation in study populations, case definition, case ascertainment sources, and surveillance methodologies among studies. CDC has been funding state health departments to establish or enhance population-based surveillance of FAS since 1997. As a result of these efforts, a standardized, multiple-source surveillance methodology was developed and implemented by the funded states. Currently, CDC funds three FAS surveillance programs located in Arizona, Colorado, and New York. HIV incidence can be used to appropriately target prevention resources to those most vulnerable to HIV transmission, identify masked or missed sub-epidemics, closely monitor for the emergence of new sub-epidemics of HIV in the general population, and measure the impact of HIV prevention programs. Several approaches have been used for measuring HIV incidence, yet each presents specific challenges. These main approaches include direct measurement in cohort studies, inference from HIV prevalence measurements in cross-sectional surveys using mathematical models, and a laboratory-based approach using tests for recent infection (TRI) in cross-sectional surveys. TRIs can be used to estimate national HIV-1 incidence in general populations in resource-constrained settings by applying the assay to specimens derived from national population based surveys with HIV testing (e.g. Demographic Health Surveys + and AIDS Indicator Surveys). Temporal trends in national HIV-1 incidence estimates can be subsequently assessed in countries that apply the assay in two or more consecutive rounds of national population based surveys. Temporal trends in HIV-1 incidence among target populations can also be tracked by applying the TRI to populations with three or more rounds of HIV sentinel surveillance. Finally, impact of PEPFAR supported HIV prevention programming can be measured using the TRI assay, through application of the assay to the targeted population at various cross-sectional points in time, including pre and post prevention programming.", `reportFrequency`:"", `shortName`:"", `specialPopulations`:"", `startDate`:"", `startDateGeneration`:"1985", `state`:"", `surveillanceDomain`:"", `surveillanceType`:"", `webResource`:"https://www.pepfar.net/C7/C8/HIV%20Incidence/default.aspx"})

Medical countermeasures (MCMs) envisioned to be distributed via MERIS may have limited or no commercial availability, and qualifiers are subject to specific uses under FDA-regulated mechanisms (Investigational New Drug Protocols, Emergency Use Authorizations, approvals). PRAMS collects state-specific, population-based data on maternal attitudes and experiences before, during, and shortly after pregnancy. PRAMS was initiated in 1987 because infant mortality rates were no longer declining as rapidly as they had in prior years. In addition, the incidence of low birth weight infants had changed little in the previous 20 years. Research has indicated that maternal behaviors during pregnancy may influence infant birth weight and mortality rates. The goal of the PRAMS project is to improve the health of mothers and infants by reducing adverse outcomes such as low birth weight, infant mortality and morbidity, and maternal morbidity. PRAMS provides state-specific data for planning and assessing health programs and for describing maternal experiences that may contribute to maternal and infant health. PRAMS provides data for state health officials to use to improve the health of mothers and infants. PRAMS allows CDC and the states to monitor changes in maternal and child health indicators (e.g., unintended pregnancy, prenatal care, breast-feeding, smoking, drinking, infant health). PRAMS enhances information from birth certificates used to plan and review state maternal and infant health programs. The PRAMS sample is chosen from all women who had a live birth recently, so findings can be applied to the state's entire population of women who have recently delivered a live-born infant. Additionally, it contributes to the knowledge base that supports the development and dissemination of appropriate public health messages and educational materials for the general public and health care providers, as well as informing federal and state policy. In 2012, the CDC Office of Public Health Genomics developed a three-tiered framework for classifying genomic tests and family health history based on the availability of scientific evidence and evidence-based recommendations supporting their use. Tier 1 genomic applications are recommended for clinical use by evidence-based panels, based on systematic review of evidence of validity and utility. Tier 2 applications may be useful for informed decision making in clinical practice, based on demonstrated validity, and promising utility.  Tier 3 applications are not ready for clinical use, due to validity or utility not demonstrated, or systematic assessment finding harms outweigh benefits. States are assessing awareness and use of specific Tier 1, 2 and 3 genomic applications using state-added questions in the Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System (BRFSS). Ethical, legal and social issues:  The potential for genetic discrimination is a concern relevant to the increased availability of genetic information associated with the increased use of genetic tests in clinical practice and in the direct-to-consumer market.  The Genetic Information Nondiscrimination Act (GINA) was signed into law in 2008. While GINA is designed to protect against discrimination in health insurance coverage and employment, it does not address other areas where discrimination is possible, such as in life, disability and long-term care insurance. More information is needed on the extent that the general public is aware of protections offered under GINA, and is concerned about the potential for genetic discrimination, since these may prove to be significant factors affecting the uptake of genetic testing. create (_2156:`Tool` {`Notes`:"", `ageFormat`:"", `applicationPlatform`:"", `applicationSolutionType`:"", `avrCapability`:"", `city`:"", `conditionsUnderSurveillance`:"", `contactEmail`:"", `contactName`:"", `contactPhone`:"", `dataPayloadFormat`:"", `dataPayloadTransmissionType`:"", `databaseTechnology`:"", `evaluationDate`:"", `evaluationDetails`:"", `fullName`:"National Program of Cancer Registries Registry Plus", `fundingLevels`:"", `fundingSource`:"", `geographicCoverageArea`:"", `id`:"TL33", `informationValidated`:"No", `lastUpdated`:"", `name`:"National Program of Cancer Registries Registry Plus", `nationalBiosurveillanceStrategyMapping`:"", `operatingSystemPlatform`:"", `operationalStatus`:"Fully Operational and Implemented", `overallCapability`:"", `patientTrackingCapability`:"", `purpose`:"Registry Plus is a suite of Windows-based software programs used for collecting, processing, maintaining, and using cancer registry data. These software programs are made available by CDC to implement and maintain the NPCR, established by Public Law 102-515. NPCR supports the development of software to aid cancer registries at all levels of registry operations.  The software is distributed free to the public health community.  All programs are compliant with national standards and can be used separately or together for both routine and special data collection.  In addition, the applications are fully customizable for user/registry-specific needs. Registry Plus currently includes ten applications, plus various utility programs: Abstract Plus, Web Plus, eMaRC Plus, Data File Mapper Plus, Prep Plus, Central Registry System (CRS) Plus, including Tumor Linkage and Consolidation (TLC) Plus, Link Plus, Registry Plus Online Help, EditWriter, and GenEDITS Plus. The NPCR Registry Plus suite of applications offers central cancer registries reporting solutions for all levels of data reporting sources. The online abstracting capability of Web Plus is suitable for reporting from physicians� offices and other low-volume reporting sources, while the file upload feature can be used for electronic submission of data from all other reporting sources (often along with the offline abstraction capability of Abstract Plus). Web Plus also supports death certificate and pathology lab follow-back efforts.  eMaRC Plus can be used to receive and map electronic pathology lab files in HL7 format to NAACCR abstracts that can be processed into the central registry. Functionality to map Electronic Heath Records received in in CDA format is also currently being added to the program. HSPD-21 defines biosurveillance as� �the process of active data-gathering with appropriate analysis and interpretation of biosphere data that might relate to disease activity and threats to human or animal health�whether infectious, toxic, metabolic, or otherwise, and regardless or intentional or natural origin�in order to achieve early warning of health threats, early detection of health events, and overall situational awareness of disease activity. The Rapid Onset of Gastroenteritis with Unexplained Etiology (ROGUE) working group investigates foodborne diseases that may have a chemical etiology. Since the most common foodborne illnesses are due to biological organisms, these are much more commonly considered early in an outbreak. Recognition of chemical etiologies is often not made until later, and this hinders ultimate identification of responsible agents because of the timeliness with which samples must be collected. Better clues are needed to help clinicians and public health officials determine if a foodborne outbreak may have a chemical etiology. People that become ill from chemical contaminants in food may comment that the food had an unusual taste, smell or appearance. These organoleptic qualities can be important clues in identifying the types of agents that may have contaminated the food and caused illness, as well as helpful in identifying that a chemical induced foodborne illness may be occurring. Unfortunately, in the investigation of foodborne illness complaints, this type of information is seldom collected in a uniform manner.", `shortName`:"ROGUE", `startDateGeneration`:"", `surveillanceDomain`:"", `webResource`:""})

create (_2293:`Collaborative` {`Notes`:"", `ageFormat`:"", `collaborativeParticipants`:"", `conditionsUnderSurveillance`:"", `contactEmail`:"", `contactName`:"", `contactPhone`:"", `dataPayloadFormat`:"", `dataPayloadTransmissionType`:"", `dataProvidersElectronic`:"", `dataProvidersManual`:"", `dataRecipientsElectronic`:"", `dataRecipientsManual`:"", `fullName`:"WHO Collaborating Center for Rotavirus and the Agents of Viral Gastroenteritis", `geographicCoverageArea`:"", `id`:"CO28", `informationValidated`:"No", `lastUpdated`:"", `name`:"WHO Collaborating Center for Rotavirus and the Agents of Viral Gastroenteritis", `operationStartDate`:"NULL", `operationalStatus`:"Fully Operational and Implemented", `patientTrackingCapability`:"", `purpose`:"CDC�s - Rotavirus Program serves as a World Health Organization (WHO) Collaborating Center for rotavirus and other viral agents of gastroenteritis. WHO designated the Rotavirus Program a Collaborating Center beginning in 1998 and redesignation occurs every five years. The primary role of the CDC Rotavirus Collaborating Center is to support the WHO Global Rotavirus Surveillance Network that collects data on rotavirus disease burden and strain prevalence as part of efforts to introduce rotavirus vaccines globally. Globally, rotavirus is the cause of about  450,000 diarrheal deaths every year,  almost all of which occur in  developing countries. In addition, many more children will get dehydrated and require medical intervention and hospitalization. Several vaccines against rotavirus have been, or are being, developed to reduce this burden. Since 2006, two live attenuated vaccines against rotavirus have been licensed in more than 100 countries around the world and are now recommended by WHO for global use. One component of efforts to introduce rotavirus vaccines globally is the derivation of global, regional and local disease burden and strain prevalence estimates to help make the case for vaccination and to support countries decisions about rotavirus vaccine introduction.. The CDC and WHO have developed a generic protocol for standardized surveillance of rotavirus disease burden and strain prevalence, and with support from the Global Alliance for Vaccines and Immunization (GAVI) and in collaboration with CDC and country partners, WHO has recently developed a Global Rotavirus Surveillance Network made up of regional networks (about 60 countries) in the six WHO regions (Americas, Africa, European, Eastern Mediterranean, Southeast Asia and Western Pacific Regions) to collect current data.  The role of the CDC Rotavirus Program and its Collaborating Center is to provide the WHO with technical expertise and laboratory and epidemiologic support to aid in network activities. Another Rotavirus Collaborating Center from Melbourne, Australia and Rotavirus Reference Laboratories from the six WHO regions provide region-specific laboratory support. Terms of reference of the center: 1. To conduct evaluations of rotavirus vaccines in collaboration with the Department of Vaccines and Biologicals (V&B, WHO) and developing country investigators. 2. To conduct rotavirus disease and strain surveillance with support from the Childhood Vaccine Program, Program of Appropriate Technology in Health (PATH) of the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation, and V&B, WHO 3. To serve as a vaccine evaluation training center for scientists for developing and developed countries. 4. To provide assistance in characterization and referencing of viral strains from WHO supported studies. 5. To provide reference strains and assistance for implementation of new diagnostic methods in developing countries for serogrouping and serotyping of the local viral strains.", `shortName`:"", `startDateGeneration`:"", `surveillanceDomain`:"", `webResource`:""})

create (_2369:`DataStandard` {`description`:"The Standard Occupational Classification (SOC) System is a United States government system of classifying occupations. It is used by U.S. federal government agencies collecting occupational data, enabling comparison of occupations across data sets. It is designed to cover all occupations in which work is performed for pay or profit, reflecting the current occupational structure in the United States. The 2010 SOC includes 840 occupational types.[1]   Users of occupational data include government program managers, industrial and labor relations practitioners, students considering career training, job seekers, vocational training schools, and employers wishing to set salary scales or locate a new plant.   The SOC codes have a hierarchical format, so for example the code \"15-0000\" refers to occupations in the \"Computer and Mathematical Occupations\" category, and \"15-1130\" is a subset for \"Software Developers and Programmers.\" [2]   The SOC does not categorize industries or employers. There are orthogonal category systems for industries used with SOC data, most commonly NAICS.
